{"uri": "8241943960", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-24", "time": "20:58:34", "dateTime": "2024-07-24T20:58:34Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-24T20:57:09Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.4313725531101227, "url": "https://www.abc.net.au/news/2024-07-25/olympics-rugby-7s-france-usa/104038560", "title": "When the USA beat France at rugby in the Olympic Games  --  and the Aussie who helped them do it", "body": "The Australian men's team could meet France in Thursday's quarterfinals depending on their final pool stage matches.\n\nThe Paris 2024 Olympic Games may symbolically open along the River Seine on Friday.\n\nHowever, so packed is the sporting action set to take place over the next couple of weeks that the entertainment started a couple of days earlier, with the highly anticipated Rugby 7s on Wednesday in front of a packed Stade de France crowd of around 70,000.\n\nAs it did so, a fascinating piece of sporting symmetry played out.\n\nIt is only a faint historical reflection, admittedly, but it's still there, peeking out in plain sight in the third game of the entire tournament as the USA fought to a battling 12-12 draw with hosts and one of the tournament favourites, France.\n\nIn fact, these two obscure rivals will also meet in Pool C of the women's competition after the opening ceremony, as well as the men's.\n\nThey are match-ups that takes place a century on from one of the most acrimonious contests in rugby history.\n\nABC Sport will be live blogging every day of the Paris Olympics from July 27\n\nRugby played an important role in the early Olympic movement.\n\nBaron Pierre de Coubertin was a big fan of rugby and its amateur ethos, learned from his trips to Britain's public schools, particularly Rugby School.\n\nNot only did de Coubertin set up the organisation that ran the French rugby championship, he refereed the first ever final in 1892, where Racing Club de France, now Racing 92, beat Stade Fran\u00e7ais 4-3.\n\n\"What is admirable in football [rugby], is the perpetual mix of individualism and discipline,\" De Coubertin said.\n\n\"The necessity for each man to think, anticipate, take a decision and at the same time subordinate one's reasoning, thoughts and decisions to those of the captain.\n\n\"And even the referee's whistle stopping a player for a 'fault' one team mate has made and he hasn't seen, tests his character and patience.\n\nRugby union featured at the Games in 1900, 1908, 1920 and 1924, directly as a result of de Coubertin's insistence, something that earned him a posthumous spot in the World Rugby Hall of Fame in 2007.\n\nIt was not always well received though -- the official report of the 1924 Games noted that although rugby-football had been designated a \"compulsory sport\" by the Comit\u00e9 Olympique de Lausanne, it \"did not achieve the hoped-for success\".\n\nThat being said, a total of 41,268 supporters attended the three matches in 1924, 21,389 of them for the final.\n\nFrance won the first, three-team tournament at the Paris Games of 1900, beating Moseley Wanderers, representing Great Britain, in the final.\n\nThat French team featured Haitian player Constantin Henriquez, the first known black player to compete -- and win -- at a Games.\n\nIn 1908, the team representing Australasia defeated Great Britain -- represented by Cornwall -- in the final (and indeed only game of the two-team tournament) 32-3 at the White City Stadium.\n\nThe Olympics was just a small part of a lengthy tour of Britain and North America for a team that would go on to be known as the first Wallabies -- indeed, the Australians had already beaten Cornwall 18-5 at Camborne earlier in the tour.\n\nAmong the tourists was a 21-year-old livewire winger from St George Rugby Club, Dan Carroll, who would go on to play just two Tests for the Wallabies.\n\nHe scored a double at the White City Stadium against Cornwall -- and went on to play a major role when the sport returned to the Games in 1920.\n\nAt the Antwerp Games of 1920 there were again just two teams, this time France and the USA, the latter featuring a number of players that had never played the sport before.\n\nAmong them though, was Carroll, who had stayed in the United States following a tour in 1912, enrolling in Stanford University in California to study geology, after which he would work in the oil industry.\n\nHe also fought in World War I, was a fierce advocate for the sport of rugby in the United States amidst the growing popularity of American Football.\n\nThe US team had been permitted to compete but only if they funded themselves on account of the team being almost entirely based just in the State of California.\n\nTheir fundraising endeavours and arduous journey were well worthwhile -- they won 8-0 against the French.\n\nThat French team were, admittedly, not the greatest French team that had ever been gathered -- they only finished fourth in that year's Five Nations Championship with a solitary victory against Ireland in Dublin.\n\nThe French were, none the less, upset at having been defeated and were honour-bound to invite the USA to the tournament again four years later.\n\nAnd so, back the Americans came, taking on France and Romania at the 1924 Games.\n\nWhy so few teams? The British Lions were on tour in South Africa, ruling out England, Scotland, Ireland -- all of whom finished above France in that year's Five Nations -- Wales and the South Africans from competing, while Australia and New Zealand cited the high costs of travelling.\n\nAs for America's Australian, there appears to be some conjecture about whether Carroll was present in Paris.\n\nSome sources, including Rugby Australia, note Carroll as a non-playing member of the coaching group in 1924, but he is not named as a member of the backroom staff in the Games official report.\n\nWhether they were joined by their two-time capped Wallaby or not, the USA did not get a pleasant welcome to France when they returned in 1924.\n\nAs was the case at this year's Olympics, the rugby competition in 1924 actually got underway before the Games officially started, taking place in May. The remainder of the competitions took place in July.\n\nIt didn't start particularly well for the Americans.\n\nAfter playing some warm up games in Britain, the team embarked on a stormy crossing of the Channel, only to initially be denied entry at Boulogne-sur-Mer.\n\n\"We were in no mood for the unpleasant reception we received at the hands of the officials of the country who was supposedly entertaining the world's athletes,\" US team manager Sam Goodman wrote.\n\n\"There was no one to meet us; our baggage was seized and for a time we were not even allowed to land on French soil, simply because the French Olympic Committee had not taken care of our visas.\"\n\nThe Americans, reportedly, took matters into their own hands -- player Norman Cleaveland said the team 'near sparked a riot\" by bundling their way off the ship, leading the local press to brand them \"street fighters and saloon brawlers.\"\n\nThings got worse -- the team were insulted everywhere they went and even had their belongings ransacked and valuables taken while training.\n\nWith France smashing Romania 59-3, and the Americans doing likewise 37-0, all was set for a defacto decider in the mini-tournament's final match.\n\nIncidentally, Romania's third-place finish was the country's first ever Olympic medal.\n\nThe level of hostility the Americans experienced from the 21,389-strong crowd at the Stade de Colombes was unprecedented, and not helped by injuries to backs Jean Vaysse and Adolphe Jaur\u00e9guy caused by robust American tackling.\n\nThe French, frustrated by the physical play of the Americans, resorted to the sort of violence that plagued the game in France and led, in 1932, to their expulsion from the Five Nations championship -- a measure bought upon the French Union amidst allegations of professionalism and on-field violence that saw 18-year-old Agen winger Michel Pradie die on the field.\n\nFrench captain Ren\u00e9 Lasserre twice punched American centre Dick Hyland -- it was only the American's protestations to Welsh referee Albert Freethy that kept him from being sent off.\n\nThat all likely made the 17-3 victory in the defacto final against the France the all the sweeter for the Americans, even if it led to widespread booing and other unpleasant scenes in the stands, with police needed to assist the players off the pitch and into the safety of the dressing rooms.\n\nIt took a long time for rugby to return to the Olympic festival, 92 years in fact before Rio hosted the first Olympic sevens tournament.\n\nThe French and American women's teams met in the fifth-place final in Rio -- the only time to date that the Americans have crossed paths with the French at an Olympics since the 1924 -- with America winning 19-5.\n\nThat is America's best finishing position at a Games, while France has a silver medal to its name in the women's competition in Tokyo.\n\nFrance's men finished 7th in Rio, while the American men have recorded finishes of ninth and sixth at Rio and Tokyo respectively.\n\nThe two sides have met frequently throughout the SVNS Series this season, with France just edging the victory count in the women's (3-2) and men's (3-1), setting up what should be a tight contest at the Stade de France.\n\nFrance were, however, winners of the series ending SVNS finals series in Madrid, with the women finishing runners up to Australia, suggesting they could be teams to beat on home soil.\n\nIn Stephen Parez, the French have one of the most in-form players on the circuit this year, as well as wild card rookie of the year Antoine Dupont, a former World Rugby player of the year in 15s.\n\n\"Antoine Dupont is the best player in the world, we think,\" France skipper Paulin Riva said after France won the Madrid title.\n\nThe Americans can, in 38-year-old one-time Philadelphia Eagles draftee Perry Baker, count on one of the greatest Sevens try-scorers in history and still prolific even at his advanced years.\n\nThe two men's teams met on day one at the Games and, in something of a surprise to the 70,000 plus raucous supporters inside the famous Stade de France stadium, the two teams could not be separated, locked at 12-12.\n\nFortunately, the Stade de France crowd responded better than their predecessors did so at the Stade de Colombes, roaring on their heroes with even more gusto in the second match against Uruguay.\n\nHowever, the hosts were clearly under extraordinary pressure and it showed, with Uruguay pushing Les Bleus all the way in a 19-12 win, not helped by the late sin binning of French forward Andy Timo.\n\nThat was in stark contrast to the Australians, who comfortably beat Kenya 21-7 and edged Samoa 21-14. The Aussies play Argentina on Thursday,\n\nThe women's competition takes place over the weekend, and is set for just as much excitement.\n\nThe USA women's team have a fascinating mix of experience and youthful exuberance, with stars like Ilona Maher helping spread the appeal of the sport Stateside via her huge presence on social media, where she has half a million followers on Instagram and over a million on TikTok.\n\nBoth teams will go in fancying their chances, but there are plenty of other teams with the same aspirations, including defending champions Fiji (men) and New Zealand (women), Australia, of course -- and given the history, let's not rule out the USA either.", "source": {"uri": "abc.net.au", "dataType": "news", "title": "Australian Broadcasting Corporation"},
